Very few people make it through the reckoning, for one reason: Instead of feeling our emotions and getting curious, we offload them onto others. We literally take that ball of emotional energy welling up inside us and hurl it toward other people. Iâ€™m going to share the six most common offloading strategies from Rising Strong. As you read through them, ask yourself two questions: Do I do this? and How does it feel to be on the receiving end of this?&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Offloading Strategy #1: Chandeliering&#13; We think weâ€™ve packed the hurt so far down that it canâ€™t possibly resurface, yet all of a sudden, a seemingly innocuous comment sends us into a rage or sparks a crying fit. Or maybe a small mistake at work triggers a huge shame attack. Perhaps a colleagueâ€™s constructive feedback hits that exquisitely tender place, and we jump out of our skin.&#13; I learned the term chandelier from Steve. Itâ€™s used within the medical community to describe a patientâ€™s pain that is so severe that if you touch that tender place, their response is involuntary. No matter how hard they try to hide the hurt or how distracted they are by other things, they jump up to the ceiling, or chandelier.&#13; The chandeliering Iâ€™m describing is the emotional equivalent, and itâ€™s especially common and dangerous in â€œpower-overâ€ situations: in environments where, because of power differentials, people with a higher position or status are less likely to be held accountable for flipping out or overreacting. This type of volatility creates distrust and disengagement.&#13; For example, someone might maintain their prized stoicism in front of customers or other people they want to impress or influence, but the second theyâ€™re around people over whom they have emotional, financial, or physical power, they explode. And because itâ€™s not a behavior seen by many of the higher-ups, their version of the story is usually perceived as truth. We see power-over chandeliering in families, churches, schools, communities, and offices. And when you mix in issues like gender, class, race, sexual orientation, or age, the combination can be toxic.&#13; Most of us have been on the receiving end of such outbursts. Even if we have the insight to know that our boss, friend, colleague, or partner blew up at us because something tender was triggered, and even when we know itâ€™s not actually about us, it still shatters trust and respect. Living, growing up, working, or worshipping on eggshells creates huge cracks in our sense of safety and self-worth. Over time, these cracks can be experienced as trauma, whether this happens at work or at home.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Offloading Strategy #2: Bouncing Hurt&#13; Pain is hard, and itâ€™s easier to be angry or pissed off than to acknowledge hurt, so our ego intervenes and does the dirty work. The ego doesnâ€™t own stories or want to write new endings; it denies emotion and hates curiosity. Instead, the ego uses stories as armor and alibi. The ego says â€œFeelings are for losers and weaklings.â€&#13; Like all good hustlers, our ego employs crews of ruffians in case we donâ€™t comply with its demands. Anger, blame, and avoidance are the egoâ€™s bouncers. When we get too close to recognizing an experience as an emotional one, these three spring into action. Itâ€™s much easier to say â€œI donâ€™t give a damnâ€ than it is to say â€œIâ€™m hurt.â€&#13; The ego likes blaming, finding fault, making excuses, inflicting payback, and lashing out, all of which are ultimately forms of self-protection. The ego is also a fan of avoidanceâ€”assuring us that weâ€™re fine, pretending that it doesnâ€™t matter, that weâ€™re impervious. We adopt a pose of indifference or stoicism, or we deflect with humor and cynicism. Whatever. Who cares? None of this matters anyway.&#13; When the bouncers are successfulâ€”when anger, blame, and avoidance push away real hurt, disappointment, or painâ€”our ego is free to scam all it wants. Often the first hustle is shaming others for their lack of â€œemotional control.â€ The ego can be a conniving and dangerous liar when it feels threatened.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Offloading Strategy #3: Numbing Hurt&#13; We talked a lot about numbing in the section on the armory. The important thing to note here is that in addition to numbing being a popular form of armor, we can offload emotion through it as well.&#13; &#13; &#13; Offloading Strategy #4: Stockpiling Hurt&#13; Thereâ€™s a quiet, insidious alternative to chandeliering, bouncing, or numbing hurtâ€”we can stockpile it. Weâ€™re not erupting with misplaced emotions or using blame to deflect our true feelings or numbing the pain. Stockpiling starts like chandeliering, by firmly packing down the pain, but instead of unleashing it on another person, we just continue to amass hurt until our bodies decide that enough is enough. The bodyâ€™s message is always clear: Shut down the stockpiling or Iâ€™ll shut you down. The body wins every time. Midlife and midcareer are when we often start to see the effects of having stockpiled emotion for too long. The body is holding down the emotional fort, and as a result, we can experience many symptoms including anxiety, depression, burnout, insomnia, and physical pain.&#13; &#13; &#13; Offloading Strategy #5: The Umbridge&#13; I named this strategy after J. K. Rowlingâ€™s character Dolores Umbridge in Harry Potter and the Order of the Phoenix, and I find it to be one of the most difficult offloading strategies to experience. Played brilliantly by Imelda Staunton in the films, Umbridge wears cutesy pink suits and pillbox hats, adorns her pink office with bows and trinkets decorated with kittens, and is a fan of torturing children who misbehave. Rowling writes about her, â€œA love of all things saccharine often seems present where there is a lack of real warmth or charity.â€&#13; Too many cheery claims, like â€œEverything is awesomeâ€ or â€œI just never really feel angry or upsetâ€ or â€œIf youâ€™re just positive, you can turn that frown upside downâ€ often mask real pain and hurt. Whatâ€™s true but seems counterintuitive is that we donâ€™t trust people who donâ€™t struggle, who donâ€™t have bad days or hard times. Itâ€™s disturbing. I learned the term because my grandmotherâ€™s driveway in San Antonio was two parallel cement strips with a mound of dirt and grass in the middle. Every now and then, my grandmother would say, â€œThe dirt and grass are getting too high. Iâ€™m gonna get high-centered in my car,â€ and weâ€™d dig out and flatten that center strip with a shovel. I donâ€™t want to cry at work, or on the battlefield, or when Iâ€™m with my students. Getting high-centered can be the worst because we feel a total loss of control. We feel powerless.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; STRATEGIES FOR RECKONING WITH EMOTION&#13; This is going to sound weird, but the most effective strategy for staying with emotion instead of offloading it is something I learned from a yoga teacher. And from a few members of the military Special Forces. Itâ€™s breathing. The yoga teacher called it box breathing. The soldiers called it tactical breathing. Turns out theyâ€™re the same thing. Former Green Beret Mark Miller explains tactical breathing this way:&#13; &#13; &#13; Inhale deeply through your nose, expanding your stomach, for a count of four.&#13; &#13; &#13; Hold in that breath for a count of four.&#13; &#13; &#13; Slowly exhale all the air through your mouth, contracting your stomach, for a count of four.&#13; &#13; &#13; Hold the empty breath for a count of four.&#13; &#13; &#13; We donâ€™t take enough deep breaths at work. We donâ€™t pause enough and check our body. Iâ€™m a breath holder, and sometimes, when things get really hectic and Iâ€™m in firefighting mode at the office, I just stop and trace a square on my desk: In for four, hold for four, out for four, hold for four. I swear just two or three of these breathing sessions rewires me. I even taught my kids and students how to do it. Breathing is also the key to another strategy for reckoning with emotion, and one of the most underrated leadership superpowers: practicing calm.&#13; I define calm as creating perspective and mindfulness while managing emotional reactivity. Calm is a superpower because it is the balm that heals one of the most prevalent workplace stressors: anxiety. When it comes to anxiety, my greatest teacher is psychologist Harriet Lerner. In her book The Dance of Connection, Dr. Lerner explains that we all have patterned ways of managing anxiety; some of us respond by overfunctioning and others by underfunctioning. Overfunctioners, like myself, tend to move quickly to advise, rescue, take over, micromanage, and get in other peopleâ€™s business rather than look inward. Underfunctioners tend to get less competent under stress. They sometimes invite others to take over and often become the focus of family gossip, worry, or concern. They can get labeled as the â€œirresponsible oneâ€ or the â€œproblem childâ€ or the â€œfragile one.â€ Dr. Lerner explains that seeing these behaviors as patterned responses to anxiety, rather than truths about who we are, can help us understand that we can change. For those of us who overfunction, our work is to become more willing to embrace our vulnerabilities in the face of anxiety. For folks who underfunction, the goal is to work on amplifying strengths and competencies.&#13; &#13; Whether we over- or underfunction, practicing calm creates the clearing we need to get emotionally grounded. The bad news is that anxiety is one of the most contagious emotions that we experience. This explains why anxiety can so easily become a function of groups, not individuals. Itâ€™s too contagious to stay contained in one person. Weâ€™ve all had the experience of one person sending a group into a tailspin.&#13; The good news? Calm is equally contagious. Over the past twenty years, the most proficient practitioners of calm that Iâ€™ve interviewed all talked about the important (and weird) combination of breathing and curiosity. They talked about taking deep breaths before responding to questions or asking them; slowing down the pace of a frantic conversation by modeling slow speech, breathing, and fact finding; and even intentionally taking a few breaths before asking themselves a version of these two questions:&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Do I have enough information to freak out about this situation?&#13; &#13; &#13; If I do have enough data, will freaking out help?&#13; &#13; &#13; In addition to curiosity and breathing, donâ€™t forget permission slips. Sometimes we have to give ourselves permission to feelâ€”especially if we come from a family where exploring and discussing emotion was either explicitly off-limits or just not modeled.&#13; Imagine how different my conversation with Steve would have been if I had paid attention to my anger and hurt, taken a few deep breaths, and become curious.&#13; &#13; &#13; The Rumble: Conspiracies, Confabulations, and Shitty First Drafts&#13; If the reckoning is how we walk into a tough story, the rumble is where we go to the mat with it and own it.&#13; The rumble starts with this universal truth: In the absence of data, we will always make up stories. Itâ€™s how we are wired. Meaning making is in our biology, and when weâ€™re in struggle, our default is often to come up with a story that makes sense of whatâ€™s happening and gives our brain information on how best to selfprotect. And it happens a hundred times a day at work. Our organizations are littered with stories that people make up because they donâ€™t have access to information. If youâ€™ve ever led a team through change, you know how much time, money, energy, and engagement bad stories cost.&#13; Robert Burton, a neurologist and novelist, explains that our brains reward us with dopamine (that â€œahaâ€ moment) when we recognize and complete patterns. Stories are patterns. The brain recognizes the familiar beginning-middle-end structure of a story and rewards us for clearing up the ambiguity. Unfortunately, the brain rewards us for a good storyâ€”one with clear good guys and bad guysâ€”regardless of the accuracy of the story.&#13; &#13; The promise of that Aha! Iâ€™ve solved it! sensation can seduce us into shutting down the uncertainty and vulnerability that are often necessary for getting to the truth. The brain is not a big fan of ambiguous stories that leave unanswered questions and a big tangle of possibilities. The brain has no interest in Maybe I have a part or Am I blowing this out of proportion? The part of the brain that goes into protection mode likes binaries: Good guy or bad guy? Dangerous or safe? Ally or enemy?&#13; Burton writes, â€œBecause we are compelled to make stories, we are often compelled to take incomplete stories and run with them.â€ He goes on to say that even with a half story in our minds, â€œwe earn a dopamine â€˜rewardâ€™ every time it helps us understand something in our worldâ€”even if that explanation is incomplete or wrong.â€&#13; The first story we make up is what we call the â€œshitty first draft,â€ or the SFD. (If youâ€™re not comfortable with shitty, I call it the â€œstormy first draftâ€ with kids. They totally get this concept and love talking about their SFDs because, after a hard experience, it gives them the opportunity to confirm that we love them and that they still belong.)&#13; The idea of a â€œshitty first draftâ€ comes from Anne Lamottâ€™s exceptional book on writing, Bird by Bird. She writes:&#13; &#13; The only way I can get anything written at all is to write really, really shitty first drafts. The first draft is the childâ€™s draft, where you let it all pour out and then let it romp all over the place, knowing that no one is going to see it and that you can shape it later.&#13; &#13; When it comes to our emotions, the first stories we make upâ€”our SFDsâ€”are definitely our fears and insecurities romping all over the place, making up worst-case scenarios. For example, Steve is a total jerk. He doesnâ€™t think Iâ€™m capable of running my business and being a great partner and mother. Heâ€™s sick of me and the stress. The past thirty years have been a giant lie.&#13; &#13; Instead of plowing into the kitchen like a bull in an emotional china shop, I wish I had noticed my reaction to the ham comment and become curious about the emotions enveloping me. Had I taken the time to surface my SFD, I could have walked in and said, â€œI heard the ham comment, and the story Iâ€™m telling myself is that youâ€™re sick of me and all of the stress of my work right now.â€&#13; Iâ€™ve known Steve for more than thirty years, and Iâ€™m 99 percent confident that he would have pulled me in for a hug and said, â€œI know youâ€™re overwhelmed. What can we do?â€&#13; Yes, the conflict worked out okay, but relationships can take only a certain amount of what I pulled in the kitchen before theyâ€™re affected by it.&#13; In our SFDs, fear fills in the data gaps. What makes that scary is that stories based on limited real data and plentiful imagined data, blended into a coherent, emotionally satisfying version of reality, are called conspiracy theories. Yes, we are all conspiracy theorists with our own stories, constantly filling in data gaps with our fears and insecurities.&#13; In work cultures where thereâ€™s a lot of change and confusion afoot, teams go crazy with SFDs. However, if you are operating in a culture of courage, you give people as many facts as you can, and when you canâ€™t tell them everything, you acknowledge that youâ€™re telling them as much as you can and that you will continue to keep them in the loop with information as you have access to it and have permission to share. Clear is kind. And clarity absolutely reduces story making and conspiracy theories.&#13; Daring leaders ask for SFDs. They create the time, space, and safety for people to reality-check their stories. In the past, when weâ€™ve had to let people go, weâ€™ve met privately with the immediate team affected, made the announcement to the larger group, then invited people to come see us during a blocked-out time to â€œtalk, ask questions, and check SFDs.â€ Keep in mind: You can spend a reasonable amount of time attending to feelings and fears (and conspiracy theories), or you can squander an unreasonable time managing unproductive behaviors.&#13; &#13; In addition to attending to conspiracy theories, we also have to watch for confabulations.&#13; Confabulation has a really great and subtle definition: A confabulation is a lie told honestly. To confabulate is to replace missing information with something false that we believe to be true.&#13; In his book The Storytelling Animal, Jonathan Gottschall explains that thereâ€™s growing evidence that â€œordinary, mentally healthy people are strikingly prone to confabulate in everyday situations.â€ In one of my favorite studies described in his book, a team of psychologists asked shoppers to choose a pair of socks from a set of seven pairs and then to give their reasons for choosing that particular pair. Every shopper explained their choice based on subtle differences in color, texture, and stitching. No shopper said, â€œI donâ€™t know why this is my choice,â€ or â€œI have no idea why I picked that one.â€ All of them had a full story that explained their decision. But hereâ€™s the kicker: All of the socks were identical. Gottschall explains that all of the shoppers told stories that made their decisions seem rational. But they really werenâ€™t. He writes, â€œThe stories were confabulationsâ€”lies, honestly told.â€&#13; Confabulation shows up at work when we share what we believe is factual information, but itâ€™s really just our opinion. Itâ€™s when I look at my colleague and say, â€œWe are all getting laid off in September. This whole group is being shut down and let go.â€ Everyone panics and asks me how I know. â€œI know, I heard, I know itâ€™s true.â€&#13; &#13; The information might have no basis in truth, none at all; itâ€™s a confabulation. I believe itâ€™s true, but itâ€™s really my fear, combined with what might be a little bit of data. And itâ€™s dangerous.&#13; With the SFD, we need to stop and capture that first story, that conspiracy, that confabulation, that scribbled mess in our heads. â€œOh, my God, she looked at me like that in the meeting because she doesnâ€™t trust me. She thinks my ideas are stupid, and she is probably plotting to get me taken off this project.â€&#13; It is incredibly important to grab hold of those before the myth making gets completely out of control. Today, I try to use my phone to capture my SFD before I act on it. I write it out when I have the opportunity simply because 70 percent of the risers we interviewed write down their SFDs. Nothing elaborate, just some variation of:&#13; The story Iâ€™m making up:&#13; My emotions:&#13; My body:&#13; My thinking:&#13; My beliefs:&#13; My actions:&#13; James Pennebaker, a researcher at the University of Texas at Austin, has found that because our minds are designed to try to understand things that happen to us, translating messy, difficult experiences into language essentially makes them â€œgraspable.â€ Storytelling is another vehicle for sharing the story youâ€™re making up. If you have a friend or colleague you trust who has the skills and patience to listen, you can talk through your SFD.&#13; Writing down your SFD doesnâ€™t give it powerâ€”it gives us power. It gives us the opportunity to say, â€œDoes this even make sense? Does this look right?â€ Writing slows the winds and calms the seas. And if youâ€™re completely mortified by the thought of someone finding your SFD because itâ€™s blamey, pissy, immature, and a full-on rant, youâ€™ve done it well. Unfiltered is powerful when it comes to the SFD.&#13; &#13; The author Margaret Atwood writes,&#13; &#13; When you are in the middle of a story, it isnâ€™t a story at all, but only a confusion; a dark roaring, a blindness, a wreckage of shattered glass and splintered wood; like a house in a whirlwind, or else a boat crushed by the icebergs or swept over the rapids, and all aboard are powerless to stop it. Itâ€™s only afterwards that it becomes anything like a story at all. When you are telling it, to yourself or to someone else.&#13; &#13; To move from what Atwood calls â€œa wreckage of shattered glass and splintered woodâ€ to a true story that you can address, these are the questions that risers need to rumble with:&#13; &#13; &#13; 1. What more do I need to learn and understand about the situation?&#13; What do I know objectively?&#13; What assumptions am I making?&#13; &#13; &#13; 2. What more do I need to learn and understand about the other people in the story?&#13; What additional information do I need?&#13; What questions or clarifications might help?&#13; Now we get to the more difficult questionsâ€”the ones that take courage and practice to answer.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; 3. What more do I need to learn and understand about myself?&#13; Whatâ€™s underneath my response?&#13; What am I really feeling?&#13; What part did I play?&#13; Answering #1 and #2 means having the courage to address the conspiracies and confabulations. Answering #3 requires emotional literacyâ€”being able to recognize and name emotionsâ€”the same skill set required in empathy and self-compassion.&#13; Imagine how powerful it would be to catch ourselves making up an SFD, rumble with it for a few minutes, then check it out with a colleague: â€œHey. Tough meeting today. You were quiet, and Iâ€™m making up that you were pissed about your team having to do all of the work for the next sprint. Can we talk about that?â€&#13; FYI: If you walked up to me and said that, my trust and respect for you would skyrocket.&#13; Letâ€™s say my response is â€œNo, Iâ€™m not mad at all. Iâ€™m exhausted. Charlieâ€™s sick and he was throwing up all night. But I appreciate you checking in.â€ This gives you the opportunity to practice empathy: â€œIâ€™m sorry. Thatâ€™s hard. Can I get you a cup of coffee?â€&#13; Now letâ€™s walk through the situation of this alternate reply: â€œYes. Iâ€™m super frustrated! This is not our project and we donâ€™t have the resources to own the work. Itâ€™s total bullshit.â€ This gives you the opportunity to say, â€œOkay. Letâ€™s sit down and talk about it.â€&#13; Win-win. Either way, this is connecting and trust-building. It sounds like a cure for lunatic behavior, but this making up stories and conspiracy theories is something we all do. Gottschall writes, â€œConspiracy is not limited to the stupid, the ignorant, or the crazy. It is a reflex of the storytelling mindâ€™s compulsive need for meaningful experience.â€ The problem is that rather than rumbling with vulnerability and staying in uncertainty, we start to fill in the blanks with our fears and worst-case-scenario planning. I love this line from Gottschall: â€œTo the conspiratorial mind, shit never just happens.â€&#13; &#13; The power of â€œthe story Iâ€™m telling myselfâ€ is that it reflects a very real part of what it means to be a meaning-making human. Itâ€™s disarming because itâ€™s honest. We all do it. This is why it works across diverse environments and with all people.&#13; For example, we recently facilitated the Daring Leadership program at Shell, with an elite deep-sea engineering team called SURF (Subsea Umbilical Risers and Flowlines).&#13; Gwo-Tarng Ju, or GT as heâ€™s more commonly known, bravely led his executive leadership team through the work. Like GT, who has a Ph.D. in aerospace engineering, most of the leaders were engineers or project managers. Part of the focus of our work together was examining how their leaders give performance feedback or lead setback debriefs as directives rather than facilitating conversations that lead to a deeper understanding of skill gaps, communication issues, and structural barriers.&#13; After spending time drilling down on the differences between systemic vulnerability (which is not good) and relational vulnerability (which is a prerequisite for courageous leadership), the team started building skills that enabled them to engage in difficult conversations with one another and their direct reports. Speaking to this new skill set, GT writes:&#13; &#13; We have been able to achieve more constructive performance feedback sessions after learning the rising skills of reality-checking the stories we all make up during conflicts or setbacks. Circling back also allows us to gain clarity and minimize the negative emotion that often frames the feedback process. By surfacing dilemmas swiftly and constructively, leaders can help resolve conflicts in a timely manner. This is critically important given the complex and high-risk environments where we work.&#13; Without real conversation around feedback, there is less learning and more defensiveness. Because itâ€™s human nature to turn on some level of self-protection when dealing with setbacks and receiving feedback, itâ€™s important to circle back with employees to ensure that the intention of the message matched what was actually heard, and to reality-check SFDs.&#13; And for organizations that use a forced ranking system in employee evaluations, itâ€™s essential to create a culture in which circling back and checking out stories is safe and built into the evaluation process. You can do this by scheduling two meetingsâ€”one for the initial conversation and one for the story checking.&#13; Another example comes from Melinda Gates, whom you met earlier. Melinda is someone who often sees curiosity and asking the right questions as a leadership superpower, and her story resonated deeply with me. She writes:&#13; &#13; For the longest time, the story I was making up was, That expert is ignoring me or condescending to me because Iâ€™m not Bill. But after years of feeling that sting, I started to realize that something else was underneath. I was worried that I didnâ€™t know enough science to lead world-renowned experts in global health. And it kept me from asking questions and from fully engaging. I was feeling like an imposter in a new field in which I didnâ€™t have a degree.&#13; Once I was able to face the fact of my insecurity, I was able to start chipping away at it. What I now believe is, I know just the right amount: enough to ask good questions, and not so much as to be distracted by minute details. Rewriting that story means that I feel confident asking seemingly â€œstupidâ€ questions, because Iâ€™ve learned that they are rarely stupid and often the most important ones to raise.&#13; Both of these are great examples of finding the courage to own hard stories so we can write new endings.&#13; In addition to affecting trust and connection in relationships and teams, the stories we tell ourselves can also crush our self-worth. The three most dangerous stories we make up are the narratives that diminish our lovability, divinity, and creativity.&#13; The reality check around our lovability: Just because someone isnâ€™t willing or able to love us, it doesnâ€™t mean that we are unlovable.&#13; The reality check around our divinity: No person is ordained to judge our divinity or to write the story of our spiritual worthiness.&#13; The reality check around our creativity: Just because we didnâ€™t measure up to some standard of achievement doesnâ€™t mean that we donâ€™t possess gifts and talents that only we can bring to the world. And just because someone failed to see the value in what we can create or achieve doesnâ€™t change its worth or ours.&#13; &#13; &#13; THE DELTA&#13; The differenceâ€”the deltaâ€”between what we make up about our experiences and the truth we discover through the process of rumbling is where the meaning and wisdom of this experience live. The delta holds our key learningsâ€”we just have to be willing to walk into our stories and rumble.&#13; In the ham fold-over debacle, I had to rumble with shame, vulnerability, and trust. My key learnings: (1) When Iâ€™m struggling and things are falling apart, Iâ€™m much more likely to shame and blame myself. I canâ€™t think of even one instance where Steve has done that to me. (2) I have to get better at asking for help. (3) I sometimes offload emotionâ€”Iâ€™m especially good at bouncing hurt with anger.&#13; &#13; Because I had the â€œIâ€™m making this upâ€ tool and was starting to put what I had learned researching Rising Strong into practice, Steve and I were able to take an angry almost-fight during an extremely stressful time and turn it into a moment of connection and trust.&#13; As we start to integrate what we learn from the Learning to Rise process into our lives, we get better at rumbling. In our office, we probably check the stories weâ€™re making up with each other ten times a day. Now itâ€™s shortened to â€œIâ€™m making up that theyâ€™re still holding the redline because their lawyers havenâ€™t reviewed it yet,â€ or â€œIâ€™m making up that no one is going to want to sit through that presentation on Friday afternoon.â€ Itâ€™s so much more honest, vulnerable, and disarming than making proclamations that are really just conjecture.&#13; Personally, I have found that sometimes the Learning to Rise process takes five minutes to get from â€œfacedown in the arenaâ€ to the delta to key learningsâ€”but sometimes it takes five days, and for the big life stuff it can take months. The more you practice rumbling with vulnerability, the better and faster you get.&#13; When we own a story and the emotion that fuels it, we get to simultaneously acknowledge that something was hard while taking control of how that hard thing is going to end. We change the narrative. When we deny a story and when we pretend we donâ€™t make up stories, the story owns us. It drives our behavior, and it drives our cognition, and then it drives even more emotions until it completely owns us.&#13; &#13; &#13; THE STORY RUMBLE&#13; One of the most useful applications of the Learning to Rise process is how we can use it when an organization, or a group within an organization, experiences a conflict or a failure or a fall. We call this the Story Rumble.&#13; &#13; Everyone who reads this book and puts the work into practice will have the basic tools for the Story Rumble. If necessary, you can even train people to facilitate the process or bring in one of our Certified Dare to Lead Facilitators to help. Weâ€™ve used this in the wake of failure to understand and address growing frustration and resentment on a team and across teams, and, most recently, to get to the bottom of a major project stall.&#13; This is the Story Rumble process: Bring as many of the courage-building tools, skills, and practices weâ€™ve discussed into the room as you canâ€”especially shared language, curiosity, grounded confidence, your integrity, your values, and the trust youâ€™re building. Youâ€™ll need them all, and youâ€™ll marvel at how they pay off.&#13; &#13; &#13; Letâ€™s set the intention for the rumble and make sure we are clear about why weâ€™re rumbling.&#13; &#13; &#13; What does everyone need to engage in this process with an open heart and mind? Container-building is important, even if thereâ€™s established trust in the group.&#13; &#13; &#13; What will get in the way of you showing up?&#13; &#13; &#13; Hereâ€™s how we commit to showing up: from #2 and #3.&#13; &#13; &#13; Letâ€™s each share one permission slip. More container- and trust-building.&#13; &#13; &#13; What emotions are people experiencing? Letâ€™s put it out there, and letâ€™s name emotions.&#13; &#13; &#13; What do we need to get curious about? Building more trust and grounded confidence by staying curious.&#13; &#13; &#13; What are your SFDs? The Turn &amp; Learn is very helpful here. These are vulnerable rumbles, and having someone with more influence go first, versus having everyone write their thoughts down and put them up on the wall at the same time, can change the outcome for the worse.&#13; &#13; &#13; What do our SFDs tell us about our relationships? About our communication? About leadership? About the culture? About whatâ€™s working and whatâ€™s not working? Stay curious, learn to resist needing to know.&#13; &#13; &#13; Where do we need to rumble? What lines of inquiry do we need to open to better understand whatâ€™s really happening and to reality-check our conspiracy theories and confabulations?&#13; &#13; &#13; Whatâ€™s the delta between those first SFDs and the new information weâ€™re gathering in the rumble?&#13; &#13; &#13; What are the key learnings?&#13; &#13; &#13; How do we act on the key learnings?&#13; &#13; &#13; How do we integrate these key learnings into the culture and leverage them as we work on new strategies? What is one thing each of us will take responsibility for embedding?&#13; &#13; &#13; When is the circle-back? Letâ€™s regroup so we can check back in and hold ourselves and one another accountable for learning and embedding.&#13; &#13; &#13; Own the story and you get to write the ending. Deny the story and it owns you.&#13; &#13; &#13; The Revolution&#13; Iâ€™m not afraid of the word revolution, Iâ€™m afraid of a world thatâ€™s becoming less courageous and authentic. Iâ€™ve always believed that in a world full of critics, cynics, and fearmongers, taking off the armor and rumbling with vulnerability, living into our values, braving trust with open hearts, and learning to rise so we can reclaim authorship of our own stories and lives is the revolution. Courage is rebellion.&#13; &#13; In fact, in 2010, I wrote:&#13; &#13; Revolution might sound a little dramatic, but in this world, choosing authenticity and worthiness is an absolute act of resistance. Choosing to live and love with our whole hearts is an act of defiance. Youâ€™re going to confuse, piss off, and terrify lots of peopleâ€”including yourself. One minute youâ€™ll pray that the transformation stops, and the next minute youâ€™ll pray that it never ends. Youâ€™ll also wonder how you can feel so brave and so afraid at the same time. At least thatâ€™s how I feel most of the timeâ€¦brave, afraid, and very, very alive.&#13; &#13; If you asked me to boil down everything Iâ€™ve learned from this research, I would tell you these three things:&#13; &#13; &#13; The level of collective courage in an organization is the absolute best predictor of that organizationâ€™s ability to be successful in terms of its culture, to develop leaders, and to meet its mission.&#13; &#13; &#13; The greatest challenge in developing brave leaders is helping them acknowledge and answer their personal call to courage. Courage can be learned if weâ€™re willing to put down our armor and pick up the shared language, tools, and skills we need for rumbling with vulnerability, living into our values, braving trust, and learning to rise.&#13; &#13; &#13; We fail the minute we let someone else define success for us. Like many of you, I spent too many years taking on projects and even positions, just to prove I could do it. I was driven by a definition of success that didnâ€™t reflect who I am, what I want, or what brings me joy. It was simply accomplish-acquire-collapse-repeat. There was very little joy, very little meaning, and tons of exhaustion and resentment.&#13; &#13; In The Gifts of Imperfection, I wrote about the importance of a â€œjoy and meaningâ€ list and the power of actually thinking through these questions: When things are going really well in our family, what does it look like? What brings us the most joy? When are we in our zone?â€ For my family, the answers included things like sleep, working out, healthy food, cooking, time off, weekends away, going to church, being present with the kids, a sense of control over our money, date nights, meaningful work that doesnâ€™t consume us, time to piddle, time with family and close friends, giving back, and time to just hang outâ€”real white space.&#13; What was shocking for me and Steve was comparing this list to how we had defined success: There was no time for joy and meaning because we were too busy achieving. And we were achieving so we could buy more joy and meaning, but those require time, and timeâ€”that precious unrenewable resourceâ€”is not for sale.&#13; Make your joy and meaning list and make sure that you use it as you define success for yourself. I stray from my list way too often, and Iâ€™m still adding to itâ€”itâ€™s a lifelong practice. But itâ€™s been the best filter for making choices when bright and shiny opportunities come my way. Now, I can ask myself if taking something on moves me closer to what brings me joy and meaning. This alone is a revolutionary act.&#13; As you think about your own path to daring leadership, remember Joseph Campbellâ€™s wisdom: â€œThe cave you fear to enter holds the treasure you seek.â€ Own the fear, find the cave, and write a new ending for yourself, for the people youâ€™re meant to serve and support, and for your culture. Choose courage over comfort. Choose whole hearts over armor. And choose the great adventure of being brave and afraid. At the exact same time.&#13; &#13; &#13;
